Thus for n=3, k=2 is impossible.

The maximum number of points that 3 sunny lines can cover is at most 3 * (maximum number of points a sunny line can contain within T). But a sunny line could contain up to n points? For n=3, a line like y=2x passes through (1,2) and (2,4) but (2,4) not in T (since a+b=6>4). So only (1,2) is in T. So it contains 1 point. Thus for n=3, possible k: 0,1,3. So k=2 is not possible.

Now, we need to determine all possible k for general n≥3. So we need to characterize which k can appear.

Observation: The covering condition is that for each point (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1, at least one of the lines containing it (row, column, diagonal) is in the set of lines. But we are not restricted to using only those families; we can use other lines. However, any line that is not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1 can be used to cover points that are not covered by the selected rows/columns/diagonals.

We need to find necessary conditions on k and sufficient constructions.

Let's denote the set of lines L. For each point (a,b), define its "type" as the set of lines that contain it from the three families: H(a,b) = {y=b}, V(a,b) = {x=a}, D(a,b) = {x+y = a+b}. If we have a line L that is not one of these families, it can contain at most one point from each row and each column, but it can contain many points if it passes through multiple lattice points that are not all on the same row/column. However, note that if a line contains two points (a,b) and (a',b') with a≠a', b≠b', then the slope is (b'-b)/(a'-a). Since all points have integer coordinates, the slope is rational. But we must avoid slopes 0, ∞, -1. So any rational slope other than 0, ∞, -1 is allowed. So a sunny line can contain multiple points from T, as long as the line through them has slope not 0, ∞, -1. But is it possible for a sunny line to contain many points from T? For example, consider the line y = 2x. For n large, it contains points (1,2), (2,4), (3,6), ... but we need a+b ≤ n+1. For a given n, the points on y=2x satisfy a+2a=3a ≤ n+1 => a ≤ floor((n+1)/3). So it can contain about n/3 points. Similarly, line y = 3x contains a+3a=4a ≤ n+1 => a ≤ floor((n+1)/4). So it can contain about n/4 points. So a sunny line can cover many points, but not as many as a diagonal line (which can cover up to n points). However, we have only n lines total, and we need to cover N = n(n+1)/2 points. If we use sunny lines, they can cover many points, but we might need to use many sunny lines to cover all points. The question is: For which k (number of sunny lines) can we achieve a covering? We need to find all k that are achievable.

We need to consider the possibility of using lines that are not from the three families. But note that the three families are special because they contain many points. The other lines can be designed to pass through specific lattice points. However, we have a limited number of lines. The covering condition is essentially that the set of lines must be such that every point is on at least one line. This is a combinatorial covering problem.

We can think of the points as edges of a bipartite graph? Another approach: Represent each line as a set of points. The condition that a point is covered if at least one of its three lines is selected is similar to a covering of the triangle by lines of three directions. But we can also use other lines that are not aligned with the grid. However, these other lines can be chosen to pass through a subset of points, possibly covering multiple points that are not collinear in the grid directions. But note: If we have a line that is not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1, it can contain at most one point from each row and each column, but it can contain multiple points from different rows and columns. So it's like a "transversal" of the grid. However, the points in T are not all in a rectangular grid; they are in a triangular shape. But we can still consider the lines.

We need to find the possible numbers of sunny lines. Let's try to derive necessary conditions.

But we need to determine all k for general n≥3. Let's analyze more systematically.

We have n lines. The covering condition: For each point (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1, there is at least one line containing it. The lines can be any. But we can think of the set of lines as a covering of the triangular lattice.

We need to find all k such that there exists a set of n lines with exactly k sunny lines covering T.

Observation: The non-sunny lines are those parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. So they are "axis-aligned" in some sense. They are natural candidates for covering many points. The sunny lines are those that are not parallel to these three directions. They can also cover points, but they are less efficient in terms of covering many points because they cannot be aligned with the grid boundaries. However, we can design sunny lines to pass through many lattice points if they have rational slopes, but the maximum number of points from T they can contain is limited by the triangle shape.

Let's denote the set of points T = { (a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1 }.

We need to cover T with n lines. Each line can be thought of as a set of points. The lines are distinct.

We need to find possible numbers of sunny lines.

First, note that we can always use the n diagonals (x+y = d for d=2..n+1). That gives k=0. So k=0 is always possible.

Now, can we have k=1? For n=3,4,5 we found constructions. In general, we can take the n-1 diagonals (all except one), and then a sunny line through the missing diagonal's points? Wait, if we take all diagonals except one, say we omit the diagonal x+y = n+1? Actually, the diagonals are x+y = d for d=2..n+1. If we take all diagonals except one, we cover all points except those on the omitted diagonal. The omitted diagonal has n points (since d=n+1 gives points (1,n),(2,n-1),...,(n,1)). So we have n-1 lines covering N - n points. Then we need to cover the n points on the omitted diagonal. We have one line left (the sunny line). Can a single line cover all n points on the diagonal x+y = n+1? That line would be the diagonal itself, which is not sunny. So we cannot use that line because it's not sunny. But we can use a sunny line that passes through all those n points? That would require the line to be the diagonal, which is not sunny. So a single sunny line cannot cover all n points on the diagonal. However, we could choose a different set of non-sunny lines. For k=1, we need exactly one sunny line. The other n-1 lines are non-sunny. So we need to cover all points with n-1 non-sunny lines and 1 sunny line. The non-sunny lines can be horizontal, vertical, or diagonal. We need to see if we can choose n-1 non-sunny lines such that together with a sunny line they cover T.

We can try to use all diagonals except one, and then a sunny line that covers the missing diagonal's points? Now, can we have k=2? For n=3,4,5 we found impossible. Let's analyze general n.

We need to cover T with n lines, exactly 2 of which are sunny. So we have n-2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are from H,V,D. They can cover points. The sunny lines can cover additional points. We need to see if there exists a configuration.

Let’s denote the set of points T. The non-sunny lines are "axis-aligned" in the sense that they are horizontal, vertical, or diagonal with slope -1. They are the only lines that can contain many points from T (since they are aligned with the grid). Sunny lines can contain at most some number of points, but they cannot contain as many as the diagonals. However, we might use sunny lines to cover points that are not covered by the non-sunny lines.

The key is to find necessary conditions on the number of points that can be covered by n-2 non-sunny lines. Since we have n lines total, and we want to cover N points, the average coverage per line is N/n = (n+1)/2. So each line on average covers about n/2 points. The non-sunny lines can cover up to n points each (if they are diagonals x+y = n+1, they cover n points). But they can also cover fewer. With n-2 non-sunny lines, the maximum number of points they can cover (distinct) is at most (n-2)*n? But they can overlap. We need to see if we can cover all points with n-2 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines. The sunny lines can cover some points, but they might not be able to cover many points because they are limited.

We need to find a necessary condition on k. Perhaps we can use a double counting argument with the three families. For each point (a,b), consider its three lines: horizontal (y=b), vertical (x=a), diagonal (x+y=a+b). If we have a set of lines L, then for each point, at least one of these three lines is in L. This is similar to a covering of the points by lines of these three directions. But we are not restricted to using only these three families; we can also use other lines. However, any line that is not from these families can contain at most one point from each horizontal line and each vertical line, but it can contain multiple points from different rows and columns. But note that if a point is covered by a sunny line, it is not necessarily covered by any of its three grid lines. So the condition that each point is on at least one line in L is weaker than requiring that each point is on at least one of its three grid lines.

We can think of the set of lines L as a hitting set for the set of points. But we need to characterize possible numbers of sunny lines.

Another approach: Consider the set of all lines that are not sunny. They are horizontal, vertical, or diagonal. They are special because they are "natural" lines that contain many points. The sunny lines are "transversal" lines that can contain at most one point from each row and each column. So they can cover points that are not collinear in the grid directions.

We need to find for which k there exists a set of n lines covering T. Since we can always use all diagonals (k=0), and we can replace some diagonals with sunny lines, but we must maintain coverage.

Thus we need to cover T_{k+1} with k sunny lines. So the problem reduces to: For which k (nonnegative integer) does there exist k lines, none of which is parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0, such that every lattice point (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ k+1 lies on at least one of these lines? And we also need that the lines are distinct.

Now, note that we are not restricted to using only the grid lines; we can use any sunny lines. So we need to find for which k we can cover the triangle of size k+1 with k lines that are not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. And we also need to ensure that the total number of lines is n, but we are only considering the sunny lines here; the non-sunny lines are the ones we already used to cover the rest. However, we also need to consider that the non-sunny lines are from H,V,D and we need to ensure that they are distinct from the sunny lines. But we can always choose the non-sunny lines to be the largest diagonals (which are distinct from any sunny line we choose, as long as we avoid those directions). So the existence of a covering for T_{k+1} with k sunny lines is sufficient to construct a configuration for the original problem with k sunny lines (by taking the n-k largest diagonals and the k sunny lines). But we also need to consider that the sunny lines might intersect the non-sunny lines, but that's fine.

Thus the problem reduces to: Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist k distinct lines in the plane, none of which is horizontal, vertical, or slope -1, that cover all lattice points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ k+1.

Thus it seems that for k≥5, the total number of points in T_{k+1} is k(k+1)/2, and the maximum points per sunny line is floor((k+1)/2). But we also have that the number of lines that can achieve that maximum is limited. In fact, for m even, the line y=x gives m/2 points. Are there other lines that also give m/2 points? For m=4, we had lines with 2 points (other than y=x). For m=6, only y=x gives 3 points. For m=8, only y=x gives 4 points. For m=10, only y=x gives 5 points. For k=7, m=8, we have y=x (4 points). Could there be other lines with 4 points? We saw y=x+1 gives 4 points? Check: y=x+1, points (1,2),(2,3),(3,4),(4,5) — (4,5) sum9≤8? No, 4+5=9>8, so not in T_8. So only a=1..3? Actually, for m=8, a+ (a+1)=2a+1≤8 => a≤3.5, so a=1,2,3, giving 3 points. Thus the only possible k for which T_{k+1} can be covered by k sunny lines are k=0,1,3. But we also need to consider k=0 is trivially possible. So for any n≥3, the possible k are 0,1,3? However, we must also check if k=3 is always possible for n≥3. For n=3, we have k=3 possible. For n=4, we have k=3 possible. For n=5, we have k=3 possible. For n=6, we have k=3 possible. So yes, for any n≥3, we can take the n-3 largest diagonals (which are non-sunny) and the 3 sunny lines covering T_4. But we need to ensure that the n-3 diagonals are distinct from the sunny lines. The sunny lines we use are y=x, line through (1,2) and (3,1), line through (1,3) and (2,1). These lines are not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. They are sunny. The diagonals we use are x+y = d for d from 4 to n+1? For n=3, n-3=0, so no diagonals. For n=4, we use diagonals d=5? Actually, we need n-3=1 diagonal: the largest diagonal is x+y = n+1 =5? That's a diagonal with slope -1, non-sunny. But does it intersect the sunny lines? That's fine. So we can use that diagonal. For n=5, we use diagonals d=6 and d=5? Actually, n-3=2, so we take the two largest diagonals: x+y=6 and x+y=5. Those are non-sunny. The sunny lines are as before. So the configuration works. So k=3 is always possible.

Now, what about k=2? We already argued impossible for all n≥3. But we need to check if there is any possibility of using a different set of non-sunny lines that are not the largest diagonals? Our reduction assumed we take the largest diagonals to maximize coverage, but maybe we can use a mix of horizontals and verticals to cover more points? However, the maximum union from n-2 non-sunny lines is N-3, as we argued. Could it be that with n-2 non-sunny lines, we can cover N-2 points? Let's analyze more generally.

We have n lines total. We want exactly k sunny lines. So we have n-k non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are from H,V,D. The union of these lines must cover at least N - (k * max points per sunny line)? Actually, we need to cover all points. The sunny lines can cover some points, but the non-sunny lines must cover the rest. The maximum number of points that can be covered by n-k non-sunny lines is at most? Since each non-sunny line covers at most n points (for diagonals x+y=n+1, they cover n points). But they can overlap. The total points in T is N = n(n+1)/2. So if we have t = n-k non-sunny lines, the maximum union size is at most t*n, but that's not tight. We need to see if we can cover N-1 points with t lines. For t = n-2, we saw that the maximum union is N-3. Could it be that for some n, we can get N-2? For n=4, t=2, max union=7, N=10, so N-3=7. For n=5, t=3, max union=12, N=15, N-3=12. For n=6, t=4, max union=18, N=21, N-3=18. So it seems the maximum union is N-3. Is it possible to get N-2? That would require covering all but 2 points. For n=5, N=15, covering 13 points. But we saw max union is 12. Could we get 13 by using a horizontal and a vertical and a diagonal? For n=5, y=1 (5 points), x=1 (5 points), d=6 (5 points) gives union 12. What about y=1 and x=2 and d=6? y=1:5, x=2:4 (points (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4) but (2,4) sum6, (2,5) sum7>5? Possibly by using two diagonals and two horizontals? So 18. What about using d=7, d=6, d=5, and y=1? That's 4 lines: d7:6, d6:5, d5:4, y1:6. Actually, pairwise intersections: (x1,y1), (x1,d7), (x1,d6), (y1,d7), (y1,d6), (d7,d6) none. So 5 overlaps? But some might coincide? (1,1) is on x1 and y1; (1,5) on x1 and d6; (1,6) on x1 and d7; (4,1) on y1 and d6; (5,1) on y1 and d7. So 5 distinct overlaps. Sum =6+6+6+5=23. Union =23-5=18. So 18. So seems max is 18. So N-2 not achievable. So indeed, the maximum union from n-2 non-sunny lines is N-3. So with k=2, we need to cover the remaining 3 points with 2 sunny lines, which is impossible.

Thus k=2 is impossible.

Now, what about k=4? For n=4, k=4 would mean all 4 lines sunny. But we already saw that 4 sunny lines can cover at most 8 points, need 10. So impossible. For n=5, k=4 would mean 4 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line. The non-sunny line can cover at most 5 points (if it's a diagonal x+y=6). Then we need 4 sunny lines to cover the remaining 10 points. But as we argued, for T_5 (size 10), the maximum points per sunny line is 2 (since m=5, floor(5/2)=2). So 4 sunny lines can cover at most 8 points, insufficient. So k=4 impossible. For n=6, k=4 would mean 4 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines. The 2 non-sunny lines can cover at most 11 points (as we saw). Then we need 4 sunny lines to cover the remaining 10 points. For T_5, max 2 points per sunny line, so 4*2=8<10. So impossible. For n=7, k=4 would mean 4 sunny lines and 3 non-sunny lines. The 3 non-sunny lines can cover at most 12 points? For n=7, N=28, max union from 3 non-sunny lines is N-6=22? Actually, the maximum union from t non-sunny lines is N - t(t+1)/2? Wait, we need to compute the maximum union from t lines from H,V,D. The best is to take the t largest diagonals (since they cover many points). The union of the t largest diagonals is sum_{d=n+2-t}^{n+1} (d-1) = sum_{i=3}^{n} i? Let's compute properly: For given n, the diagonals are d=2..n+1. The size of diagonal d is d-1. The t largest diagonals are d = n+1, n, ..., n+2-t. Their union size is sum_{d=n+2-t}^{n+1} (d-1) = sum_{i=n+1-t}^{n} i? Actually, let j = d-1, then j ranges from n+1-t to n? For d=n+1, j=n; d=n, j=n-1; ... d=n+2-t, j=n+1-t. So union = sum_{j=n+1-t}^{n} j = (sum_{j=1}^{n} j) - (sum_{j=1}^{n-t} j) = n(n+1)/2 - (n-t)(n-t+1)/2. That's the number of points covered by the t largest diagonals. So the uncovered points are those with a+b ≤ n+1-t? Since the smallest d is n+2-t, so points with sum ≤ n+1-t are not covered. That's exactly the triangle T_{n+1-t} with size (n+1-t)(n+2-t)/2? Wait, the number of points with a+b ≤ m is m(m+1)/2. So with m = n+1-t, the count is (n+1-t)(n+2-t)/2. So the uncovered points count is (n+1-t)(n+2-t)/2. For t = n-k, this is (k+1)(k+2)/2? Let's compute: n+1-t = n+1-(n-k)=k+1. So uncovered points = (k+1)(k+2)/2. So the maximum union from t non-sunny lines is N - (k+1)(k+2)/2. For k=4, t=n-4, uncovered points = 5*6/2=15. So the non-sunny lines cover N-15 points. Then we need 4 sunny lines to cover those 15 points. The maximum points per sunny line in that set is floor((k+1)/2)=floor(5/2)=2? Actually, the uncovered set is T_{k+1}=T_5, which has 15 points? Wait, T_5 has size 10? No, T_m has size m(m-1)/2? Let's be consistent: The uncovered set is points with a+b ≤ k+1? Since m = k+1, the number of points is sum_{d=2}^{k+1} (d-1) = k(k+1)/2. For k=4, that's 4*5/2=10. So I made a mistake: (k+1)(k+2)/2 is the number of points with a+b ≤ k+2? Let's recompute: For m = k+1, the number of points with a+b ≤ m is sum_{d=2}^{m} (d-1) = (m-1)m/2 = k(k+1)/2. So the uncovered set size is k(k+1)/2. For k=4, that's 10. So the uncovered set has 10 points. Then we need 4 sunny lines to cover 10 points. The maximum points per sunny line in that set is floor(m/2)=floor((k+1)/2)=floor(5/2)=2. So 4 sunny lines can cover at most 8 points, insufficient. So k=4 impossible.

Thus for k≥4, the uncovered set size is k(k+1)/2, and the maximum points per sunny line is floor((k+1)/2). So the total incidences from k lines is at most k * floor((k+1)/2). We need this to be at least k(k+1)/2. This gives floor((k+1)/2) ≥ (k+1)/2, which forces k+1 even, i.e., k odd. So k must be odd. But we also need that we can actually achieve the covering. For k=1,3, it works. For k=5, we need to cover 15 points with 5 lines. The maximum points per line is 3, but only one line can have 3 points. So the total coverage is at most 3 + 2*4 = 11, which is less than 15. So impossible. For k=7, we need to cover 28 points with 7 lines. The maximum points per line is 4, but only one line can have 4 points. So total coverage ≤ 4 + 3*6 = 22 < 28. So impossible. In general, for odd k≥5, the line y=x gives (k+1)/2 points. But can there be other lines with (k+1)/2 points? For k=5, (k+1)/2=3, only y=x gives 3 points. For k=7, (k+1)/2=4, only y=x gives 4 points. For k=9, (k+1)/2=5, only y=x gives 5 points. So only one line can achieve that maximum. All other lines have at most (k+1)/2 - 1 points? Actually, for k=5, other lines have at most 2 points. For k=7, other lines have at most 3 points? Let's check: For T_8, the maximum points per line other than y=x is 3? We saw y=x+1 gives 3 points (since m=8, a+ (a+1)=2a+1≤8 => a≤3.5, so a=1,2,3 gives 3 points). So max is 3. So the total coverage from k lines is at most (k+1)/2 + (k-1)*((k+1)/2 - 1) = (k+1)/2 + (k-1)*(k-1)/2? Actually, ((k+1)/2 - 1) = (k-1)/2. So total ≤ (k+1)/2 + (k-1)*(k-1)/2 = (k+1)/2 + (k-1)^2/2 = (k+1 + (k-1)^2)/2 = (k+1 + k^2 -2k +1)/2 = (k^2 -k +2)/2. We need this to be at least k(k+1)/2 = (k^2+k)/2. So we need (k^2 -k +2)/2 ≥ (k^2+k)/2 => -k+2 ≥ k => 2 ≥ 2k => k ≤ 1. So only k=1 works. But we also have k=3, which is odd and works. For k=3, (k+1)/2=2, and other lines can have 2 points as well. So the bound is not simply (k+1)/2 for the maximum; for k=3, there are lines with 2 points other than y=x. So the maximum total coverage for k=3 is 3*2=6, which equals the total points. So the covering works. For k=5, the maximum total coverage is 3 + 2*4 = 11 < 15. So fails. For k=7, 4 + 3*6 = 22 < 28. So fails. So the only odd k that work are those for which there exist k lines that together cover all points of T_{k+1}. For k=1, it's trivial. For k=3, we have a construction. For k=5, we need to see if there is any other construction that doesn't rely on covering each point exactly once? But the total points is 15, and we have 5 lines. The maximum total points covered by 5 lines is at most the sum of the sizes of the lines. Since each line can contain at most 3 points, the sum is at most 15. To cover 15 points, we need each line to contain exactly 3 points, and the lines must be disjoint (since any overlap would reduce the union size). So we need a partition of the 15 points into 5 lines, each line containing exactly 3 points. As we argued, only y=x can have 3 points. So we would need 5 lines each with 3 points, but only one such line exists. So impossible. Could we have a line that contains 4 points? For T_6, the maximum points per line is 3, so no. So k=5 impossible.

Thus the only possible k are 0,1,3.

But we must also consider if k can be n? For n=3, k=3 possible. For n=4, k=4 impossible. For n=5, k=5 impossible. So k=n only possible for n=3.

Now, we need to check if k=0 is always possible? Yes, use all diagonals.

k=1 always possible? Yes, use n-1 largest diagonals and a sunny line through (1,1). But we need to ensure that the sunny line is not parallel to forbidden directions. That's fine.

k=3 always possible? Yes, use n-3 largest diagonals and the 3 sunny lines covering T_4.

But we must also consider the condition that the lines are distinct. For n=3, n-3=0, so no diagonals. We need 3 sunny lines covering T_4. That's possible as we did. For n=4, n-3=1, we need 1 diagonal (x+y=5) and 3 sunny lines covering T_4. The diagonal x+y=5 is non-sunny. The sunny lines are y=x, line through (1,2) and (3,1), line through (1,3) and (2,1). Are these distinct from the diagonal? Yes. So works. For n=5, n-3=2, we need diagonals x+y=6 and x+y=5, and 3 sunny lines. The diagonals are non-sunny. The sunny lines are as before. They are distinct. So works. For n=6, n-3=3, diagonals x+y=7,6,5, and 3 sunny lines. Works. So for any n≥3, k=3 is possible.

Thus the answer: For n≥3, the possible k are 0, 1, 3.

But wait, we also need to consider if k=0 is allowed? The problem says "exactly k of the n lines are sunny". So k can be 0. Yes.

Now, we should also consider if k=2 is ever possible? We argued no. But let's double-check for n=3, we already saw k=2 impossible. For n=4, we saw impossible. For n=5, we saw impossible. For n=6, we saw impossible. So likely never.

But is there any possibility of using a different set of non-sunny lines that are not the largest diagonals, but maybe a combination that covers N-2 points? For n=5, we need 3 non-sunny lines covering 13 points. Is there a set of 3 lines from H,V,D that covers 13 points? Total points is 15. Could we cover 13? For example, take y=1 (5 points), x=1 (5 points), and a diagonal that covers many points? The union of y=1 and x=1 is 9 points (since overlap at (1,1)). Then we need a third line that covers 4 more points. The third line could be a diagonal that covers many points. For instance, diagonal x+y=5 covers 4 points, but some of those might already be covered. The points covered by y=1 and x=1: from y=1: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1); from x=1: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5). Union = (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5) — 9 points. Not 13. What about diagonal x+y=6? That covers (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). Among these, (1,5) is on x=1, (5,1) is on y=1, so adds (2,4),(3,3),(4,2) — 3 new points, total 12. Still not 13. Diagonal x+y=4? That covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). (1,3) on x=1, (3,1) on y=1, so adds (2,2) only, total 10. So max is 12. So cannot get 13. For n=6, we need 4 non-sunny lines covering 19 points. Is it possible? The maximum union from 4 lines is 18 (from 4 diagonals). Could we get 19 by using a mix? For example, take y=1 (6), x=1 (6), d=7 (6), d=6 (5). Overlaps: y1 and x1: (1,1); y1 and d7: (5,1); y1 and d6: (4,1); x1 and d7: (1,6); x1 and d6: (1,5); also d7 and d6: none; also y1 and x1 already counted. So overlaps: 5 points? Actually, we have 4 lines, pairwise intersections: (y1,x1), (y1,d7), (y1,d6), (x1,d7), (x1,d6), (d7,d6) none. So 5 distinct overlaps. Sum sizes =6+6+6+5=23. Union =23-5=18. So 18. What about using y=1, y=2, d=7, d=6? y1:6, y2:5, d7:6, d6:5. Sum=22. Overlaps: y1 with d7: (5,1); y1 with d6: (4,1); y2 with d7: (4,2); y2 with d6: (3,2); also y1 with y2: none; d7 with d6: none; so 4 overlaps. Union=22-4=18. So 18. Could we get 19 by using a different combination? Possibly using a horizontal and a vertical and two diagonals? Already did. So max 18. So N-2=19 not achievable. So indeed, the maximum union from n-2 lines is N-3.

Thus k=2 impossible.

Now, we also need to consider if k=0 is always possible? Yes, use all diagonals. But we must also check that the diagonals are distinct and sunny? No, they are non-sunny. So k=0 is possible.

Thus the answer: For any n≥3, the possible k are 0, 1, and 3.

But we should also check if k=3 is always possible for n=3? Yes, we have a construction. For n=4, we have a construction. For n=5, we have a construction. For n=6, we have a construction. So yes.

Now, we need to write a rigorous proof. The proof should include:
- Definition of sunny lines.

So with 3 non-sunny lines, we could cover all 15 points if they are disjoint. But we already argued that the maximum union from 3 lines is 12, not 15. So the sum of the sizes is not the union size; it's the sum of the number of points on each line, counting multiplicities. The union size is at most the sum, but it can be less if there is overlap. So the inequality N ≤ sum of sizes is always true, but it doesn't give a contradiction. For n=5, sum of sizes ≤ 15, so N ≤ 15 holds, but we need to show that the actual union is less than N. So we need a bound on the union.

We can use the following: For any line L from H,V,D, the number of points on L is at most n. But also, the lines are "orthogonal" in the sense that the points on different lines are arranged in a triangular grid. There is a known result: The maximum number of points of a triangular lattice that can be covered by t lines of slopes 0, ∞, -1 is achieved by taking the t lines with the largest intercepts. This is a standard result in covering problems. We can prove it by considering the "boundary" of the triangle.

We can use the fact that the points are arranged in a triangular grid. We need to solve the problem: For n ≥ 3 integer, determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both conditions:
- For all positive integers a and b with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines.
- Exactly k of the n lines are sunny. A line is sunny if it is not parallel to the x-axis, y-axis, or the line x+y=0.

Interpretation: We have a set of n distinct lines. The first condition: For every lattice point (a,b) with positive integer coordinates a,b and a+b ≤ n+1, that point must lie on at least one of the lines. So we need to cover all such points with lines. The lines are arbitrary (not necessarily through lattice points, but they must contain those points). The second condition: Exactly k of those lines are sunny, i.e., not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or line x+y=0. So lines that are parallel to any of those three directions are not sunny. Sunny lines are those that are not parallel to those three lines.

We need to find for each n≥3, which k (nonnegative integer) are possible. So we need to characterize the possible numbers of sunny lines among n lines that cover all those points.

First, note that the points (a,b) with a,b positive integers and a+b ≤ n+1 form a triangular lattice of points. There are (n+1 choose 2) such points? Actually number of positive integer pairs with a+b ≤ n+1: For a=1, b can be 1..n; a=2, b=1..n-1; ... a=n, b=1. So total = n + (n-1) + ... + 1 = n(n+1)/2. So there are N = n(n+1)/2 points to cover.

We have n lines. Each line can cover many points. The lines are distinct. We need to cover all these points. The condition about sunny lines: lines that are not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or line x+y=0. So lines that are parallel to these three directions are not sunny. The three directions: horizontal (x-axis), vertical (y-axis), and slope -1 (line x+y=0). So a line is sunny if its slope is not 0, not infinite, and not -1. So any line with slope other than 0, ∞, -1 is sunny.

We need to determine all possible k (number of sunny lines) that can appear in such a covering configuration.

Observation: The points (a,b) are arranged in a triangular grid. The lines that are not sunny are horizontal, vertical, and lines of slope -1. These lines are natural candidates for covering many points. For example, a horizontal line y = c covers all points with b = c (and a positive integer such that a+b ≤ n+1). Similarly vertical line x = c covers points with a = c. And line x+y = d covers points with a+b = d.

But note: The lines must be distinct. The covering condition: For each point (a,b) with a+b ≤ n+1, there must be at least one line that contains it. So we need to choose n lines (some may be horizontal, vertical, slope -1, or others) such that every lattice point in that triangle is on at least one line.

We have n lines, but there are many points. Usually, covering a triangular grid with lines is like covering with lines that are "axes" or "diagonals". The classic problem: Cover all points of the form (i,j) with 1≤i,j and i+j ≤ n+1 by n lines. This is reminiscent of the concept of "covering a set of points with lines" and the "sunny" condition adds a restriction.

We need to find all k such that there exists a set of n lines with exactly k sunny lines covering all points.

Let's denote the set of lines L = {L1,...,Ln}. For each line, we can classify its direction: horizontal (slope 0), vertical (infinite slope), slope -1 (i.e., direction of line x+y=0), or other (sunny). The non-sunny lines are exactly those parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0.

We need to cover all points (a,b) with a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1. Let's denote the triangle T = { (a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1 }.

Observation: The points lie on lines of the three families: horizontal lines y = b (b=1..n), vertical lines x = a (a=1..n), and lines x+y = d (d=2..n+1). Actually x+y = d for d from 2 to n+1 (since a,b≥1 => d≥2). And also note that the point (a,b) lies on exactly one horizontal line (y=b), one vertical line (x=a), and one diagonal line (x+y = a+b). So each point is at the intersection of one line from each family.

Now, if we use only lines from these three families, we can cover all points by taking all horizontal lines? But we only have n lines total. There are n horizontal lines (b=1..n) but also n vertical lines (a=1..n) and n diagonal lines (d=2..n+1) which is also n lines. So each family has exactly n lines. But we cannot take all three families because we only have n lines total. However, we can take a combination.

We need to cover all points. Consider the covering by lines of slope -1: The line x+y = d covers all points with a+b = d. So if we take all diagonal lines for d=2..n+1, that's n lines and covers all points (since each point has a+b = some d between 2 and n+1). So the set of n lines consisting of all diagonals (x+y = d for d=2..n+1) satisfies the covering condition. Are these lines sunny? The line x+y = d is parallel to x+y=0, so it is not sunny (since sunny excludes lines parallel to x+y=0). So all n lines are non-sunny. So k=0 is possible.

Similarly, we could take all horizontal lines y = b for b=1..n, that's n lines, covering all points? Does a horizontal line y=b cover points with b = b? Yes, but it covers only points with that y-coordinate. For a given b, the points (a,b) with a≥1, a+b ≤ n+1, so a ≤ n+1-b. So horizontal line covers those points. But does it cover points with different y? No. So if we take all horizontal lines, each point (a,b) lies on the horizontal line y=b. So yes, all points are covered. So k=0 again because all lines are horizontal (non-sunny). Similarly vertical lines.

So k=0 is achievable.

Now, can we have k>0? That means we include some sunny lines (slope not 0, ∞, -1). We need to cover all points with n lines. Since we have n lines, we might replace some of the non-sunny lines with sunny lines, but then we must still cover all points. We need to find for which k there exists a configuration.

We need to determine all possible numbers of sunny lines among n lines that cover T.

Let's denote H = set of horizontal lines (slope 0), V = vertical lines (infinite slope), D = lines of slope -1 (x+y = constant). Non-sunny lines are H ∪ V ∪ D. Sunny lines are all others.

We have n lines. Let h = number of horizontal lines, v = number of vertical lines, d = number of diagonal lines (slope -1), and s = number of sunny lines, so h+v+d+s = n. And k = s (exactly k sunny lines). We need to cover all points in T.

Observation: Points in T are arranged in a triangular grid. Each point lies on exactly one horizontal line, one vertical line, and one diagonal line. So if we have a set of lines, a point is covered if at least one of its three lines is in the set.

We need to cover all points. This is reminiscent of covering a bipartite graph or a combinatorial design.

We can think of the points as edges of a complete bipartite graph? Actually, consider the set of points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1. This can be seen as the set of cells in a Young diagram? Alternatively, we can consider the lines as "rows", "columns", and "diagonals". The covering condition is that for each cell (a,b) in the triangle, at least one of the row a, column b, or diagonal a+b is selected.

We have n lines total. We need to choose a set of lines (rows, columns, diagonals, and possibly other lines) such that every cell is covered. The other lines (sunny) can cover multiple cells, but they are not aligned with the grid; they can pass through some lattice points but not necessarily all. However, we can design sunny lines to pass through many points, but they are not restricted to the grid directions. Since we have freedom to choose any lines, we can choose sunny lines that pass through many points, potentially covering many points at once. But note: The points are lattice points; a line can contain at most one point from each horizontal line? Actually, a line with slope not 0, ∞, -1 can contain at most one point from each horizontal line? Because if it intersects y=b, it gives a unique x. So it can contain at most one point per y-coordinate. Similarly, at most one per x-coordinate. So a sunny line can contain at most one point from each row and each column. But it can contain many points if it passes through many lattice points? However, a line that is not horizontal/vertical/diagonal can pass through at most one lattice point? Actually, a line with rational slope can pass through multiple lattice points if the slope is rational and the line passes through lattice points. For example, line y = 2x passes through (1,2), (2,4), etc. So it can contain many lattice points. But note: Our points are only those with a+b ≤ n+1. So a sunny line could contain many such points. However, it is not necessarily aligned with the grid, so it can contain points that are not all on the same row or column. But it can contain at most one point per row (since each row has a unique y), but it can contain multiple points from different rows. So it can cover many points.

But we need to cover all N points with n lines. Since N = n(n+1)/2, which is roughly n^2/2, each line on average must cover about n/2 points. That is plausible if we use lines that are diagonals (cover exactly n+1-b points? Actually a diagonal line x+y = d covers points with a+b = d, which are exactly (d-1) points? Wait: For a fixed d, the points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b = d: a from 1 to d-1, b = d-a, so there are d-1 points. But d ranges from 2 to n+1, so the number of points on diagonal d is d-1, which varies from 1 to n. So the total points on all diagonals is sum_{d=2}^{n+1} (d-1) = n(n+1)/2, which is N. So each diagonal line covers a certain number of points. The average number of points per diagonal is (n+1)/3? Actually sum of (d-1) = N, and there are n diagonals, so average = N/n = (n+1)/2. So each diagonal covers on average (n+1)/2 points. So using all diagonals gives coverage with n lines, each covering about n/2 points. So that's a natural covering.

Now, if we replace some diagonals with other lines, we must still cover all points. The other lines can be horizontal, vertical, or sunny. Horizontal lines: y = b covers points with that y, number of points = n+1-b (since a from 1 to n+1-b). So horizontal lines cover varying numbers, average also (n+1)/2? Actually sum_{b=1}^{n} (n+1-b) = n(n+1)/2, average = (n+1)/2. Similarly vertical lines: x = a covers points with that a, number = n+1-a, average = (n+1)/2. So each of these families also average (n+1)/2 points per line. So if we use a mix of these, we can still cover all points.

But note: The covering condition must be satisfied for all points. For a given set of lines, a point is covered if at least one of its row, column, or diagonal is selected. So we need to select a set of lines (from H, V, D, and possibly others) such that every point is covered. The other lines (sunny) are not part of these families, so they can cover points in a more arbitrary way. However, they also can be used to cover points that are not covered by the selected rows/columns/diagonals. But we have a limited number of lines: n. So we need to find configurations that cover all points.

We need to determine all possible k (number of sunny lines) that can be achieved.

First, note that if we use only lines from H, V, D, then all lines are non-sunny, so k=0. So k=0 is possible.

Can we have k=1? That means exactly one sunny line, and the other n-1 lines are non-sunny (horizontal, vertical, or diagonal). We need to cover all points. Is it possible? We need to choose n-1 lines from H, V, D, and one sunny line, such that every point is on at least one of these lines. Since the sunny line can cover some points, we can try to cover the rest with the n-1 lines. But we need to ensure that the union of the selected lines covers all points.

We need to see if we can cover all points with n-1 lines from H, V, D. That would be a covering of the triangular grid by at most n-1 lines from those three families. Is that possible? The total number of lines in each family is n. If we take all horizontal lines, that's n lines and covers all points. But we only have n-1 lines, so we cannot take all horizontals. Similarly, we cannot take all verticals or all diagonals. So we need to select a subset of these families that covers all points. However, note that if we take all horizontals, we need n lines. If we take all verticals, we need n lines. If we take all diagonals, we need n lines. So with n-1 lines from these families, it's impossible to cover all points because each family individually requires n lines to cover all points. But maybe a combination of different families can cover all points with fewer than n lines? For example, take some horizontals and some verticals: The union of a set of horizontals and verticals covers all points that lie in the union of those rows and columns. But there are points that are not in any selected row or column. For instance, if we take all horizontals except one, we miss all points on that missing row. But we could cover those points with verticals? Actually, if we take all horizontals except y = b0, then the points on that row are not covered by horizontals. But we could cover them with verticals: For each point (a,b0), we need a vertical line x = a to cover it. But we have only n-1 lines total, and we already used n-1 horizontals (since we have n-1 lines total, we cannot also take many verticals). So if we take all horizontals except one, that's n-1 horizontals, then we have no lines left for verticals. So the points on the missing row are uncovered. So that fails.

What about taking a mix of horizontals and diagonals? For instance, take all diagonals except one? That's n-1 diagonals, then we miss points on that missing diagonal. Could those points be covered by horizontals? Possibly, if we also include some horizontals, but we have only n-1 lines total; if we take n-2 diagonals and 1 horizontal, we might cover some points. But we need to cover all points. Let's analyze systematically.

We have n lines. The points are in a triangle. Each point is covered if at least one of its row, column, or diagonal is selected. This is similar to covering a set of points with lines that are "rows", "columns", and "diagonals". The problem is reminiscent of the concept of "covering a triangular array with lines" and the minimum number of lines needed to cover all points. In fact, the minimum number of lines from these three families needed to cover all points is n. Because each line covers a set of points that is a "row" (horizontal), "column" (vertical), or "diagonal" (slope -1). It is known that the covering number of the triangular grid by lines of these three directions is exactly n. This can be seen by a double counting argument: For each point (a,b), consider the three lines that contain it. If we have a set of lines that covers all points, then each point is assigned to one of its three lines. This gives a covering. The sum over lines of the number of points covered by that line is at least N. But we have n lines, each line covers at most n points? Actually, a horizontal line y=b covers n+1-b points, which is at most n (when b=1, covers n points). Similarly vertical line x=a covers at most n points, diagonal x+y=d covers at most n points (when d=n+1, covers n points). So each line covers at most n points. So the total points covered (counting multiplicities) is at most n * n = n^2. But we need to cover N = n(n+1)/2 points, which is less than n^2 for n≥2. So that bound doesn't force n lines.

But is it possible to cover all points with fewer than n lines from these families? Let's try small n to see.

n=3: Points: (1,1), (1,2), (1,3), (2,1), (2,2), (3,1). That's 6 points. Can we cover all 6 points with 2 lines from H,V,D? Let's list lines:
H: y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1) -> 3 points; y=2 covers (1,2),(2,2) -> 2 points; y=3 covers (1,3) -> 1 point.
V: x=1 covers (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) -> 3 points; x=2 covers (2,1),(2,2) -> 2 points; x=3 covers (3,1) -> 1 point.

So a sunny line can contain 2 points. Also, does it contain (1,1) and (2,2) only? Yes. So that's possible.

Now, can we cover all 6 points with three sunny lines? We have three lines, each can contain at most 2 points? But maybe we can have lines that contain 2 points each, and together they cover all points. For example, take lines: y=x (covers (1,1),(2,2)), y=2x (covers (1,2)? Actually (1,2) slope 2 from origin? But line through (1,2) with slope 2: y=2x? That passes through (0,0) and (1,2) and (2,4) not in T. So only (1,2) is in T. So that covers 1 point. Another line: y=3x-2? That passes through (1,1) already covered. So we need to cover (3,1),(1,3),(2,1)? Actually points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1). If we take y=x, we cover (1,1),(2,2). Then we need to cover (1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(3,1). Could we cover these with two sunny lines? For instance, line through (1,2) and (2,1) is diagonal x+y=3, not sunny. So we need separate lines. A sunny line through (1,2) and (3,1) would have slope (1-2)/(3-1) = -1/2, which is not 0, ∞, -1. So that line is sunny. It contains (1,2) and (3,1). So that covers two points. Then we need to cover (1,3) and (2,1). Could we cover both with a sunny line? A line through (1,3) and (2,1) has slope (1-3)/(2-1) = -2, which is not 0, ∞, -1. So that line is sunny. It contains (1,3) and (2,1). So we have three sunny lines: y=x, line through (1,2) and (3,1), line through (1,3) and (2,1). That covers all points. So k=3 is possible for n=3.

Thus for n=3, possible k: 0,1,3. So k=2 is not possible.

Now, we need to determine all possible k for general n≥3. So we need to characterize which k can appear.

Observation: The covering condition is that for each point (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1, at least one of the lines containing it (row, column, diagonal) is in the set of lines. But we are not restricted to using only those families; we can use other lines. However, any line that is not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1 can be used to cover points that are not covered by the selected rows/columns/diagonals.

We need to find necessary conditions on k and sufficient constructions.

Let's denote the set of lines L. For each point (a,b), define its "type" as the set of lines that contain it from the three families: H(a,b) = {y=b}, V(a,b) = {x=a}, D(a,b) = {x+y = a+b}. If we have a line L that is not one of these families, it can contain at most one point from each row and each column, but it can contain many points if it passes through multiple lattice points that are not all on the same row/column. However, note that if a line contains two points (a,b) and (a',b') with a≠a', b≠b', then the slope is (b'-b)/(a'-a). Since all points have integer coordinates, the slope is rational. But we must avoid slopes 0, ∞, -1. So any rational slope other than 0, ∞, -1 is allowed. So a sunny line can contain multiple points from T, as long as the line through them has slope not 0, ∞, -1. But is it possible for a sunny line to contain many points from T? For example, consider the line y = 2x. For n large, it contains points (1,2), (2,4), (3,6), ... but we need a+b ≤ n+1. For a given n, the points on y=2x satisfy a+2a=3a ≤ n+1 => a ≤ floor((n+1)/3). So it can contain about n/3 points. Similarly, line y = 3x contains a+3a=4a ≤ n+1 => a ≤ floor((n+1)/4). So it can contain about n/4 points. So a sunny line can cover many points, but not as many as a diagonal line (which can cover up to n points). However, we have only n lines total, and we need to cover N = n(n+1)/2 points. If we use sunny lines, they can cover many points, but we might need to use many sunny lines to cover all points. The question is: For which k (number of sunny lines) can we achieve a covering? We need to find all k that are achievable.

We need to consider the possibility of using lines that are not from the three families. But note that the three families are special because they contain many points. The other lines can be designed to pass through specific lattice points. However, we have a limited number of lines. The covering condition is essentially that the set of lines must be such that every point is on at least one line. This is a combinatorial covering problem.

We can think of the points as edges of a bipartite graph? Another approach: Represent each line as a set of points. The condition that a point is covered if at least one of its three lines is selected is similar to a covering of the triangle by lines of three directions. But we can also use other lines that are not aligned with the grid. However, these other lines can be chosen to pass through a subset of points, possibly covering multiple points that are not collinear in the grid directions. But note: If we have a line that is not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1, it can contain at most one point from each row and each column, but it can contain multiple points from different rows and columns. So it's like a "transversal" of the grid. However, the points in T are not all in a rectangular grid; they are in a triangular shape. But we can still consider the lines.

We need to find the possible numbers of sunny lines. Let's try to derive necessary conditions.

Let’s denote the set of lines L. For each point (a,b), let f(a,b) be the number of lines in L that contain it. The condition is that f(a,b) ≥ 1 for all points. We want to count the total number of incidences (point-line pairs) in two ways: sum_{points} f(a,b) = sum_{lines} (number of points on that line). For each line, the number of points on it is at most n (since the triangle has at most n points per row/column/diagonal, but a sunny line could also have at most n points? Actually, a line with slope not 0, ∞, -1 can contain at most one point from each row (y-coordinate) and each column (x-coordinate). Since there are n possible y-values (1..n) and n possible x-values (1..n), the maximum number of points from T that a line can contain is at most n, because it can intersect at most n horizontal lines (y=1..n) and at most n vertical lines, but it could intersect all n rows? However, if it passes through a point (a,b), then for each other y, there is at most one x. But the points in T have a+b ≤ n+1. But the maximum possible is n, achieved by a line that goes through (1,1), (2,2), ..., (n,n) but that line is y=x, which is sunny, and it contains n points? Actually, points (a,a) satisfy a+b=2a ≤ n+1 => a ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So for n≥2, it contains only about n/2 points. So the maximum number of points from T that a line can contain is at most n, but it's not necessarily n. For a line like y=0? Not allowed. For a line like x+y = n+1, it contains n points (since a from 1 to n, b = n+1-a, so n points). So that's a diagonal line. For a line like y = 2x, it contains about n/3 points. So the maximum number of points a line can contain within T is n (achieved by the line x+y = n+1, which is a diagonal, non-sunny). But for sunny lines, the maximum is less than n? Possibly, the line y = x contains about n/2 points. But could there be a sunny line that contains n points? For a line to contain n points, it must intersect all n rows or all n columns. For a line with slope not 0, ∞, -1, if it contains n points, then the points must have distinct x and y coordinates, and the line must pass through n lattice points with integer coordinates. For example, the line y = 2x + c: For it to contain n points, the points must be (a, 2a+c) with a from 1 to n, but then b = 2a+c must be positive and a+b ≤ n+1. That gives a + 2a + c = 3a + c ≤ n+1. For a=n, we need 3n + c ≤ n+1 => 2n + c ≤ 1, impossible for n≥2. So a line with slope >1 cannot contain many points because the sum grows quickly. Similarly, slope between 0 and 1: y = (1/2)x + c gives a + (1/2)a + c = 1.5a + c ≤ n+1. For a=n, 1.5n + c ≤ n+1 => 0.5n + c ≤ 1, so n must be 1 or 2. So for n≥3, a line with slope not 0, ∞, -1 cannot contain n points. In fact, the maximum number of points a sunny line can contain within T is at most floor((n+1)/2) for slope 1? Actually, line y=x contains points (a,a) with a ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So about n/2. For slope 2, it's about n/3. For slope 1/2, it's about 2n/3? Wait, y = (1/2)x + c: For a from 1 to n, b = a/2 + c. To have b integer, c must be half-integer? Actually, if slope is 1/2, then the line passes through lattice points only if the line is of the form y = (1/2)x + c with c such that for integer x, y is integer. That requires c to be half-integer? For example, line through (1,1) and (3,2) has slope 1/2, and it contains points (1,1), (3,2), (5,3), ... but then a+b: (1+1)=2, (3+2)=5, (5+3)=8, etc. So the sum grows by 3 each step. So the number of points within T is about n/3? Actually, for a given n, the maximum a such that a + (a/2 + c) ≤ n+1. If c is chosen appropriately, the line can contain about 2n/3 points? Let's analyze: Suppose we have a line y = mx + c with m rational, not 0, ∞, -1. For it to contain many lattice points, the line must be such that the set of integer x for which y is integer is an arithmetic progression. The number of such points with x between 1 and n and a+b ≤ n+1 is limited. In general, the maximum number of points a line can contain in T is n (achieved by diagonal x+y = n+1). For sunny lines, the maximum is less than n because they cannot be aligned with the triangle boundary. However, we are not forced to use only lines that contain many points; we can use sunny lines that contain few points, and rely on other lines to cover the rest.

The key is to find all possible k such that there exists a set of n lines covering T. But we need to determine all k for general n≥3. Let's analyze more systematically.

We have n lines. The covering condition: For each point (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1, there is at least one line containing it. The lines can be any. But we can think of the set of lines as a covering of the triangular lattice.

We need to find all k such that there exists a set of n lines with exactly k sunny lines covering T.

Observation: The non-sunny lines are those parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. So they are "axis-aligned" in some sense. They are natural candidates for covering many points. The sunny lines are those that are not parallel to these three directions. They can also cover points, but they are less efficient in terms of covering many points because they cannot be aligned with the grid boundaries. However, we can design sunny lines to pass through many lattice points if they have rational slopes, but the maximum number of points from T they can contain is limited by the triangle shape.

Let's denote the set of points T = { (a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1 }.

We need to cover T with n lines. Each line can be thought of as a set of points. The lines are distinct.

We need to find possible numbers of sunny lines.

First, note that we can always use the n diagonals (x+y = d for d=2..n+1). That gives k=0. So k=0 is always possible.

Now, can we have k=1? For n=3,4,5 we found constructions. In general, we can take the n-1 diagonals (all except one), and then a sunny line through the missing diagonal's points? Wait, if we take all diagonals except one, say we omit the diagonal x+y = n+1? Actually, the diagonals are x+y = d for d=2..n+1. If we take all diagonals except one, we cover all points except those on the omitted diagonal. The omitted diagonal has n points (since d=n+1 gives points (1,n),(2,n-1),...,(n,1)). So we have n-1 lines covering N - n points. Then we need to cover the n points on the omitted diagonal. We have one line left (the sunny line). Can a single line cover all n points on the diagonal x+y = n+1? That line would be the diagonal itself, which is not sunny. So we cannot use that line because it's not sunny. But we can use a sunny line that passes through all those n points? That would require the line to be the diagonal, which is not sunny. So a single sunny line cannot cover all n points on the diagonal. However, we could choose a different set of non-sunny lines. For k=1, we need exactly one sunny line. The other n-1 lines are non-sunny. So we need to cover all points with n-1 non-sunny lines and 1 sunny line. The non-sunny lines can be horizontal, vertical, or diagonal. We need to see if we can choose n-1 non-sunny lines such that together with a sunny line they cover T.

We can try to use all diagonals except one, and then a sunny line that covers the missing diagonal's points? But as argued, a single sunny line cannot cover all n points on a diagonal because the only line containing all those points is the diagonal itself, which is not sunny. So we need to cover the missing diagonal's points with the sunny line plus maybe some of the other lines? But we already used all diagonals except one, so the missing diagonal's points are not covered by any of the n-1 diagonals. So they must be covered by the sunny line. So the sunny line must contain all n points on that diagonal. That's impossible unless the line is the diagonal, which is not sunny. So this approach fails.

But we can use a different combination of non-sunny lines. For example, for n=3, we used y=1, y=2, and a sunny line through (1,3). That's 2 non-sunny lines (both horizontal) and 1 sunny line. The two horizontals covered 5 points, leaving (1,3) uncovered. So the sunny line covered that one point. So the non-sunny lines did not need to cover all points except one; they covered 5 out of 6, and the sunny line covered the missing one. So the non-sunny lines can be chosen to cover all but a few points, and the sunny line covers the rest. For n=4, we used y=1, y=2, x+y=5, and a sunny line through (1,3). That's 3 non-sunny lines covering 9 points, leaving (1,3) uncovered. So the sunny line covered that one point. So in general, for k=1, we need to find n-1 non-sunny lines that cover all but at most 1 point of T, and then a sunny line through that missing point. But we have n lines total, so the n-1 non-sunny lines cover all but possibly some points, and the sunny line covers those. Since we have exactly 1 sunny line, it can cover at most 1 point if it's a single point? Actually, a sunny line can cover many points, but if we need to cover only a few missing points, it can cover them all. So we need to see if there exists a set of n-1 non-sunny lines that covers all but at most 1 point of T. For n=3, we had 2 non-sunny lines covering 5 points, leaving 1. For n=4, we had 3 non-sunny lines covering 9 points, leaving 1. For n=5, we had 4 non-sunny lines covering 14 points, leaving 1? Actually, we used diagonals 3,4,5,6? That's 4 lines covering 2+3+4+5=14 points, leaving (1,1) uncovered. So yes, 4 non-sunny lines cover 14 points, leaving 1. So in general, if we take all diagonals except the one with d=2 (which is just (1,1)), we have n-1 diagonals covering all points except (1,1). That's n-1 lines covering N - 1 points. So that works for k=1. So k=1 is always possible for n≥3? Let's check n=3: all diagonals except d=2 gives diagonals d=3,4? That's 2 lines, covering points: d=3: (1,2),(2,1); d=4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). That's 5 points, missing (1,1). So yes. So k=1 is possible for all n≥3.

Now, can we have k=2? For n=3,4,5 we found impossible. Let's analyze general n.

We need to cover T with n lines, exactly 2 of which are sunny. So we have n-2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are from H,V,D. They can cover points. The sunny lines can cover additional points. We need to see if there exists a configuration.

Let’s denote the set of points T. The non-sunny lines are "axis-aligned" in the sense that they are horizontal, vertical, or diagonal with slope -1. They are the only lines that can contain many points from T (since they are aligned with the grid). Sunny lines can contain at most some number of points, but they cannot contain as many as the diagonals. However, we might use sunny lines to cover points that are not covered by the non-sunny lines.

The key is to find necessary conditions on the number of points that can be covered by n-2 non-sunny lines. Since we have n lines total, and we want to cover N points, the average coverage per line is N/n = (n+1)/2. So each line on average covers about n/2 points. The non-sunny lines can cover up to n points each (if they are diagonals x+y = n+1, they cover n points). But they can also cover fewer. With n-2 non-sunny lines, the maximum number of points they can cover (distinct) is at most (n-2)*n? But they can overlap. We need to see if we can cover all points with n-2 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines. The sunny lines can cover some points, but they might not be able to cover many points because they are limited.

We need to find a necessary condition on k. Perhaps we can use a double counting argument with the three families. For each point (a,b), consider its three lines: horizontal (y=b), vertical (x=a), diagonal (x+y=a+b). If we have a set of lines L, then for each point, at least one of these three lines is in L. This is similar to a covering of the points by lines of these three directions. But we are not restricted to using only these three families; we can also use other lines. However, any line that is not from these families can contain at most one point from each horizontal line and each vertical line, but it can contain multiple points from different rows and columns. But note that if a point is covered by a sunny line, it is not necessarily covered by any of its three grid lines. So the condition that each point is on at least one line in L is weaker than requiring that each point is on at least one of its three grid lines.

We can think of the set of lines L as a hitting set for the set of points. But we need to characterize possible numbers of sunny lines.

Another approach: Consider the set of all lines that are not sunny. They are horizontal, vertical, or diagonal. They are special because they are "natural" lines that contain many points. The sunny lines are "transversal" lines that can contain at most one point from each row and each column. So they can cover points that are not collinear in the grid directions.

We need to find for which k there exists a set of n lines covering T. Since we can always use all diagonals (k=0), and we can replace some diagonals with sunny lines, but we must maintain coverage.

Thus the problem reduces to: Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist k distinct lines in the plane, none of which is horizontal, vertical, or slope -1, that cover all lattice points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ k+1.

Thus the possible k for a given n are exactly those k such that T_{k+1} can be covered by k sunny lines. And we also need that k ≤ n, because we have n lines total. So for each n≥3, we need to determine all k ≤ n for which there exists a covering of T_{k+1} with k sunny lines.

Now, we already have examples:
- k=0: trivially possible (no sunny lines, we can use all diagonals). But we need to check if k=0 is allowed? Yes, k is nonnegative integer.
- k=1: we need to cover T_2 (points with a+b≤2) with 1 sunny line. T_2 has points: (1,1) only? Actually, a+b≤2: (1,1) sum2; (1,2) sum3>2, so only (1,1). So we need a sunny line through (1,1). That's possible: any line through (1,1) that is not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. For example, line y=2x-1? That passes through (1,1) and (2,3) but (2,3) not in T_2, so it's fine. So k=1 is possible.
- k=2: we need to cover T_3 (points with a+b≤3) with 2 sunny lines. T_3 has points: (1,1), (1,2), (2,1). That's 3 points. Can we cover these 3 points with 2 sunny lines? As we argued, any line through two of them is forbidden. So we would need each sunny line to cover at least one point, but we have 3 points and 2 lines, so one line must cover 2 points, which is impossible. So k=2 is impossible.
- k=3: we need to cover T_4 (points with a+b≤4) with 3 sunny lines. T_4 has points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1) — 6 points. We already found a covering: y=x (covers (1,1),(2,2)), line through (1,2) and (3,1) (slope -1/2), line through (1,3) and (2,1) (slope -2). So k=3 is possible.
- k=4: we need to cover T_5 (points with a+b≤5) with 4 sunny lines. T_5 has 10 points. Can we cover these 10 points with 4 sunny lines? Each sunny line can contain at most? For T_5, the maximum number of points a sunny line can contain is 2? Actually, we need to see if there is a sunny line that contains 3 points from T_5. The points in T_5: sums 2,3,4,5. The line y=x contains (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) but (3,3) sum6>5, so only (1,1),(2,2) -> 2 points. Slope 2: points (1,2),(2,4) but (2,4) sum6>5, so only (1,2) maybe? Actually, (2,4) sum6, not in T_5; so only 1 point. Slope 1/2: points (1,1),(3,2) but (3,2) sum5, yes; (5,3) sum8>5, so 2 points. Slope 1/3: (1,1),(4,2) but (4,2) sum6>5, so only (1,1). So no line contains 3 points. So each sunny line can cover at most 2 points. With 4 lines, we can cover at most 8 points, but we need to cover 10 points. So impossible. Thus k=4 is impossible.

- k=5: we need to cover T_6 (points with a+b≤6) with 5 sunny lines. T_6 has 15 points. Can we cover these 15 points with 5 sunny lines? For slope 1, points (a,a) with a+b=2a≤6 => a≤3, so 3 points. For slope 2, points (a,2a+c) with 3a+c≤6: for a=1, 3+c≤6 => c≤3; a=2, 6+c≤6 => c≤0; so max 2 points. For slope 1/2, points (a, a/2+c) with 1.5a+c≤6: for a=1, 1.5+c≤6 => c≤4.5; a=2, 3+c≤6 => c≤3; a=3, 4.5+c≤6 => c≤1.5; a=4, 6+c≤6 => c≤0; so can have up to 4 points? - k=6: we need to cover T_7 (points with a+b≤7) with 6 sunny lines. T_7 has 21 points. In general, the maximum number of lattice points on a line within the triangle is achieved by the line y=x, which gives about m/2 points. So the maximum is floor(m/2). So for T_{k+1}, the maximum points per sunny line is floor((k+1)/2). So with k sunny lines, the maximum total points they can cover (counting multiplicities) is k * floor((k+1)/2). But we need to cover all N = (k+1)(k+2)/2 points. So we need k * floor((k+1)/2) ≥ (k+1)(k+2)/2.

In general, for T_{m} with m = k+1, the number of points is k(k+1)/2. The maximum number of points a sunny line can contain is floor(m/2) = floor((k+1)/2). Now, we need to produce a solution in the required XML format.

Let's structure the proof:

1. Preliminaries: Define T = { (a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1 }. Condition: For each point in T, at least one of the n lines contains it. Sunny lines: not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0.

2. Show k=0 possible: Take all diagonals x+y = d for d=2,...,n+1. These are n distinct lines, none sunny (since parallel to x+y=0). They cover all points because each point has a+b = d for some d.

3. Show k=1 possible: For n≥3, take the n-1 diagonals x+y = d for d=4,...,n+1 (these are non-sunny). These cover all points except those on the diagonal x+y=3? Actually, we need to cover all points. That's 2 non-sunny lines (both horizontal) and 1 sunny line. For n=4, we used y=1, y=2, and x+y=5, and a sunny line through (1,3). That's 3 non-sunny lines. So the general construction for k=1: Take all horizontals y=b for b=1,...,n-1? That would be n-1 horizontals, covering points with y from 1 to n-1. For n=4, we used y=1, y=2, and x+y=5, covering 9 points, leaving (1,3). For n=5, we used diagonals x+y=5,6,7? Actually, we used diagonals d=5,6,7? That's 3 diagonals covering 4+5+6=15 points? This can be proven by a simple greedy algorithm: Start with the line that covers the most points (the hypotenuse). Then remove the points it covers, and repeat. This yields a set of lines that are diagonals. So the maximum union is achieved by taking the t largest diagonals. Given the complexity, perhaps there is a known solution to this problem. The problem is from an olympiad (maybe IMO Shortlist). I recall a problem: "A line in the plane is called sunny if it is not parallel to the x-axis, y-axis, or the line x+y=0. Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines satisfying both: for all positive integers a and b with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines; and exactly k of the n lines are sunny." This might be from an IMO Shortlist. The answer is likely k ∈ {0,1,3}. I think I've seen a similar problem. Perhaps we can use a linear programming bound: For any set of lines, the number of points covered is at most the sum of the sizes of the lines minus the number of overlaps. Given the time, I'll try to find a more direct argument for k=2. Let's denote the two sunny lines as L1 and L2. Consider the points on the hypotenuse. Now, consider the points on the legs. There are 2n-1 points. The non-sunny lines can cover many of them. But we need to show that the total number of points covered by the n lines is at most N-1. Perhaps we can use a counting argument with the "small" points. For each point (a,b), consider the number of lines from the set that contain it. The sum of these numbers is sum_{lines} (points on that line). For the sunny lines, the number of points on each is at most floor((n+1)/2). For the non-sunny lines, it's at most n. So the total sum is at most 2*floor((n+1)/2) + n*(n-2). But that's not a bound on the union.

Maybe we can use the following: For any set of lines, the number of points covered is at most the sum of the sizes of the lines minus the number of overlaps. The sunny lines are also sparse (covering at most one point per diagonal? Actually, a line with slope not 0, ∞, -1 can intersect each diagonal at most once? So all lines cover at most one point per diagonal. Therefore, the total number of points covered on a given diagonal d is at most the number of lines that intersect that diagonal. A diagonal line covers all points on that diagonal, which is d-1 points. So diagonal lines are "dense" and cover many points on a single diagonal. So we need to treat diagonal lines differently.

Thus the non-sunny lines include diagonal lines that can cover many points on a single diagonal. So the bound on the union is not simply the sum of the number of lines intersecting each diagonal.

We were unable to produce a complete proof. However, the strongest partial progress is as follows:
- We can construct examples for \(k=0,1,3\).
- We have a lemma that the union of \(n-2\) lines from the three families (horizontal, vertical, slope \(-1\)) covers at most \(N-3\) points of \(T\).
- For \(k=2\) we have a contradiction using that lemma and a geometric argument that the uncovered set contains a triangle of size 3 that cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines.
- For \(k\ge 4\) we have not completed the argument.

But the problem asks to determine all nonnegative integers \(k\) such that there exist \(n\) distinct lines satisfying the conditions. The answer is likely \(k\in\{0,1,3\}\). So the total number of leg points that can be covered by the sunny lines is at most \(2k\). Since \(k\ge 4\), \(2k\ge 8\). For \(n=5\), \(n+1=6\), so \(8\ge 6\), possible. For \(n=6\), \(n+1=7\), \(8\ge 7\), possible. So no contradiction.
